		</div></li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 215.33333333333px"><div style="width: 215.33333333333px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 213.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Basic_Measuring_Instruments_-_Math_Processor_83002190_-_Zilog_Z80_SIO_Z84C4008PEC-3920.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Basic_Measuring_Instruments_-_Math_Processor_83002190_-_Zilog_Z80_SIO_Z84C4008PEC-3920.jpg/320px-Basic_Measuring_Instruments_-_Math_Processor_83002190_-_Zilog_Z80_SIO_Z84C4008PEC-3920.jpg" width="214" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Basic_Measuring_Instruments_-_Math_Processor_83002190_-_Zilog_Z80_SIO_Z84C4008PEC-3920.jpg/480px-Basic_Measuring_Instruments_-_Math_Processor_83002190_-_Zilog_Z80_SIO_Z84C4008PEC-3920.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Basic_Measuring_Instruments_-_Math_Processor_83002190_-_Zilog_Z80_SIO_Z84C4008PEC-3920.jpg/640px-Basic_Measuring_Instruments_-_Math_Processor_83002190_-_Zilog_Z80_SIO_Z84C4008PEC-3920.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4592" data-file-height="2583" /></a></div></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">
<p>SIO Z84C4008PEC
</p>
			</div>
		</div></li>
</ul><p>Like the 8080, 8085 and 8086 processors, but unlike processors such as the Motorola 6800 and MOS Technology 6502, the Z80 and 8080 has a separate control line and address space for I/O instructions. While some Z80-based computers such as the <a href="/wiki/Osborne_1" title="Osborne 1">Osborne 1</a> used "Motorola-style" <a href="/wiki/Memory-mapped_I/O" title="Memory-mapped I/O">memory mapped input/output</a> devices, usually the I/O space was used to address one of the many Zilog peripheral chips compatible with the Z80. Zilog I/O chips supported the Z80's new mode 2 interrupts which simplified interrupt handling for large numbers of peripherals.
</p><p>The Z80 was officially described as supporting 16-bit (64&#160;KB) memory addressing, and 8-bit (256 ports) I/O-addressing. All I/O instructions actually assert the entire 16-bit address bus. OUT (C),reg and IN reg,(C) places the contents of the entire 16 bit BC register on the address bus;<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> OUT (n),A and IN A,(n) places the contents of the A register on b8-b15 of the address bus and n on b0-b7 of the address bus. A designer could choose to decode the entire 16 bit address bus on I/O operations in order to take advantage of this feature, or use the high half of the address bus to select subfeatures of the I/O device. This feature has also been used to minimise decoding hardware requirements, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Amstrad_CPC" title="Amstrad CPC">Amstrad CPC</a>/<a href="/wiki/Amstrad_PCW" title="Amstrad PCW">PCW</a> and <a href="/wiki/ZX81" title="ZX81">ZX81</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_sources_and_derivatives">Second sources and derivatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zilog_Z80&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Second sources and derivatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_sources">Second sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zilog_Z80&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Second sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KL_Mostek_MK3880P_Z80.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/KL_Mostek_MK3880P_Z80.jpg/220px-KL_Mostek_MK3880P_Z80.jpg" width="220" height="121" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/KL_Mostek_MK3880P_Z80.jpg/330px-KL_Mostek_MK3880P_Z80.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/KL_Mostek_MK3880P_Z80.jpg/440px-KL_Mostek_MK3880P_Z80.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1380" data-file-height="762" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KL_Mostek_MK3880P_Z80.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mostek" title="Mostek">Mostek</a>'s Z80: MK3880</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KL_NEC_uPD780C.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/KL_NEC_uPD780C.jpg/220px-KL_NEC_uPD780C.jpg" width="220" height="122" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/KL_NEC_uPD780C.jpg/330px-KL_NEC_uPD780C.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/KL_NEC_uPD780C.jpg/440px-KL_NEC_uPD780C.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1419" data-file-height="789" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KL_NEC_uPD780C.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/NEC_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="NEC Corporation">NEC</a>'s μPD780C Z80 second-sourced by NEC</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sharp_LH0080A.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Sharp_LH0080A.jpg/220px-Sharp_LH0080A.jpg" width="220" height="117" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Sharp_LH0080A.jpg/330px-Sharp_LH0080A.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Sharp_LH0080A.jpg/440px-Sharp_LH0080A.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="424" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sharp_LH0080A.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sharp_Corporation" title="Sharp Corporation">Sharp</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Sharp_LH0080" class="mw-redirect" title="Sharp LH0080">LH0080</a> Sharp version of the Z80</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KL_USSR_T34BM1_Z80_Black_Background.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/KL_USSR_T34BM1_Z80_Black_Background.jpg/220px-KL_USSR_T34BM1_Z80_Black_Background.jpg" width="220" height="114" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/KL_USSR_T34BM1_Z80_Black_Background.jpg/330px-KL_USSR_T34BM1_Z80_Black_Background.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/KL_USSR_T34BM1_Z80_Black_Background.jpg/440px-KL_USSR_T34BM1_Z80_Black_Background.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1398" data-file-height="726" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KL_USSR_T34BM1_Z80_Black_Background.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/w/index.php?title=T34_(microprocessor)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="T34 (microprocessor) (page does not exist)">T34BM1</a>, a Soviet Z80 clone</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TMPZ84C015AF_01.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/TMPZ84C015AF_01.png/220px-TMPZ84C015AF_01.png" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/TMPZ84C015AF_01.png 1.5x" data-file-width="283" data-file-height="236" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TMPZ84C015AF_01.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Toshiba TMPZ84C015; a standard Z80 with several Z80-family peripherals on chip in a <a href="/wiki/QFP" class="mw-redirect" title="QFP">QFP</a> package</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HD64180_DIP.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/HD64180_DIP.jpg/220px-HD64180_DIP.jpg" width="220" height="94" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/HD64180_DIP.jpg/330px-HD64180_DIP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/HD64180_DIP.jpg/440px-HD64180_DIP.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="341" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HD64180_DIP.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Z80 compatible Hitachi <a href="/wiki/HD64180" class="mw-redirect" title="HD64180">HD64180</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:218px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Z180_PLCC_1988.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/43/Z180_PLCC_1988.png" width="216" height="175" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="216" data-file-height="175" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Z180_PLCC_1988.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Z180" class="mw-redirect" title="Z180">Z180</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Plastic_leaded_chip_carrier" class="mw-redirect" title="Plastic leaded chip carrier">PLCC</a> package</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:R800_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/R800_02.jpg/220px-R800_02.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/R800_02.jpg/330px-R800_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d1/R800_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="415" data-file-height="312" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:R800_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Z80 compatible <a href="/wiki/R800_(CPU)" title="R800 (CPU)">R800</a> in <a href="/wiki/QFP" class="mw-redirect" title="QFP">QFP</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Z280_PLCC_1987.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Z280_PLCC_1987.png/220px-Z280_PLCC_1987.png" width="220" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dc/Z280_PLCC_1987.png 1.5x" data-file-width="251" data-file-height="228" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Z280_PLCC_1987.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Z280 in a <a href="/wiki/Plastic_leaded_chip_carrier" class="mw-redirect" title="Plastic leaded chip carrier">PLCC</a> package</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Mostek" title="Mostek">Mostek</a>, who produced the first Z80 for Zilog, offered it as second-source as MK3880. SGS-Thomson (now <a href="/wiki/STMicroelectronics" title="STMicroelectronics">STMicroelectronics</a>) was a second-source, too, with their Z8400. <a href="/wiki/Sharp_Corporation" title="Sharp Corporation">Sharp</a> and <a href="/wiki/NEC_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="NEC Corporation">NEC</a> developed second sources for the <a href="/wiki/NMOS_logic" title="NMOS logic">NMOS</a> Z80, the LH0080 and µPD780C respectively. The µPD780C was used in the Sinclair <a href="/wiki/ZX80" title="ZX80">ZX80</a> and <a href="/wiki/ZX81" title="ZX81">ZX81</a>, original versions of the <a href="/wiki/ZX_Spectrum" title="ZX Spectrum">ZX Spectrum</a>, and several <a href="/wiki/MSX" title="MSX">MSX</a> computers, and in musical synthesizers such as <a href="/wiki/Oberheim_OB-8" title="Oberheim OB-8">Oberheim OB-8</a> and others. The LH0080 was used in various home computers and personal computers made by Sharp and other Japanese manufacturers, including Sony MSX computers, and a number of computers in the <a href="/wiki/Sharp_MZ" title="Sharp MZ">Sharp MZ</a> series.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Toshiba" title="Toshiba">Toshiba</a> made a <a href="/wiki/CMOS" title="CMOS">CMOS</a>-version, the TMPZ84C00, which is believed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (December 2010)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> (but not verified) to be the same design also used by Zilog for its own CMOS Z84C00. There were also Z80-chips made by <a href="/wiki/GoldStar" title="GoldStar">GoldStar</a> (now <a href="/wiki/LG_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="LG Group">LG</a>) and the BU18400 series of Z80-clones (including DMA, PIO, CTC, DART and SIO) in <a href="/wiki/NMOS_logic" title="NMOS logic">NMOS</a> and <a href="/wiki/CMOS" title="CMOS">CMOS</a> made by <a href="/wiki/ROHM_Electronics" class="mw-redirect" title="ROHM Electronics">ROHM Electronics</a>.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a>, an unlicensed clone of the Z80, known as the <a href="/wiki/U880" title="U880">U880</a>, was manufactured. It was very popular and was used in <a href="/wiki/VEB_Robotron" title="VEB Robotron">Robotron</a>'s and VEB Mikroelektronik Mühlhausen's computer systems (such as the <a href="/wiki/KC85" class="mw-redirect" title="KC85">KC85</a>-series) and also in many self-made computer systems. In <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> another unlicensed clone could be found, named <a href="/wiki/MMN80CPU" title="MMN80CPU">MMN80CPU</a> and produced by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Microelectronica&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Microelectronica (page does not exist)">Microelectronica</a>, used in home computers like TIM-S, HC, COBRA.
</p><p>Also, several clones of Z80 were created in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, notable ones being the <a href="/w/index.php?title=T34_(microprocessor)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="T34 (microprocessor) (page does not exist)">T34BM1</a>, also called <a href="/wiki/%D0%9A%D0%A01858%D0%92%D0%9C1" class="mw-redirect" title="КР1858ВМ1">КР1858ВМ1</a> (parallelling the Soviet 8080-clone <a href="/wiki/KR580VM80A" title="KR580VM80A">KR580VM80A</a>). The first marking was used in pre-production series, while the second had to be used for a larger production. Though, due to the collapse of Soviet microelectronics in the late 1980s, there are many more T34BM1s than КР1858ВМ1s.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Derivatives">Derivatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zilog_Z80&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Derivatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<dl><dt>Compatible with the original Z80</dt>
<dd></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hitachi,_Ltd." class="mw-redirect" title="Hitachi, Ltd.">Hitachi</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/HD64180" class="mw-redirect" title="HD64180">HD64180</a>, a microcoded and partially dynamic Z80 in CMOS, with on chip peripherals and a simple MMU giving a 1&#160;<a href="/wiki/Megabyte" title="Megabyte">MB</a> address space. It was later second sourced by Zilog, initially as the Z64180, and then in the form of the slightly modified <a href="/wiki/Z180" class="mw-redirect" title="Z180">Z180</a><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> which has bus protocol and timings better adapted to Z80 peripheral chips. Z180 has been maintained and further developed under Zilog's name, the newest versions being based on the fully static S180/L180 core with very low power draw and EMI (noise).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Toshiba" title="Toshiba">Toshiba</a> developed the 84 pin Z84013 / Z84C13 and the 100 pin Z84015 / Z84C15 series of "intelligent peripheral controllers", basically ordinary NMOS and CMOS Z80 cores with Z80 peripherals, watch dog timer, power on reset, and wait state generator on the same chip. Manufactured by <a href="/wiki/Sharp_Corporation" title="Sharp Corporation">Sharp</a> as well as Toshiba. These products are today second sourced by Zilog.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The 32-bit Z80 compatible Zilog <a href="/wiki/Z380" class="mw-redirect" title="Z380">Z380</a>, introduced 1994, is used mainly in telecom equipment.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li>Zilog's fully pipelined Z80 compatible <a href="/wiki/Zilog_eZ80" title="Zilog eZ80">eZ80</a><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> with an 8/16/24-bit word length and a linear 16&#160;MB address space was introduced in 2001. It exists in versions with on chip <a href="/wiki/Static_random_access_memory" class="mw-redirect" title="Static random access memory">SRAM</a> or <a href="/wiki/Flash_memory" title="Flash memory">flash memory</a>, as well as with integrated peripherals. One variant has on chip <a href="/wiki/Media_access_control" class="mw-redirect" title="Media access control">MAC (media access controller)</a>, and available software include a <a href="/wiki/TCP/IP_stack" class="mw-redirect" title="TCP/IP stack">TCP/IP stack</a>. In contrast with the Z800 and Z280, there are only a few added instructions (primarily <a href="/wiki/Load_Effective_Address" class="mw-redirect" title="Load Effective Address">LEAs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Push_Effective_Address" class="mw-redirect" title="Push Effective Address">PEAs</a>, and variable-address 16/24-bit loads), but instructions are instead executed between 2 and 11 times as clock cycle efficient as on the original Z80 (with a mean value around 3-5 times). It is currently specified for clock frequencies up to 50&#160;MHz.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kawasaki_Heavy_Industries" title="Kawasaki Heavy Industries">Kawasaki</a> developed the binary compatible KL5C8400 which is approximately 1.2-1.3 times as clock cycle efficient as the original Z80 and can be clocked at up to 33&#160;MHz. Kawasaki also produces the KL5C80A1x family, which has peripherals as well as a small RAM on chip; it is approximately as clock cycle efficient as the eZ80 and can be clocked at up to 10&#160;MHz (2006).<sup id="cite_ref-kawasaki_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kawasaki-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The NEC uPD9002 was an hybrid CPU compatible with both Z80 and x86 families.</li>
<li>The Chinese Actions Semiconductor's audio processor family of chips (ATJ2085 and others) contains a Z80-compatible MCU together with a 24-bit dedicated DSP processor.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> These chips are used in many MP3 and media player products.</li>
<li>The T80 (VHDL) and TV80 (Verilog) synthesizable soft cores are available from OpenCores.org.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dt>Non-compatible</dt>
<dd></dd></dl>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Toshiba_TLCS" title="Toshiba TLCS">Toshiba TLCS</a> 900 series of high volume (mostly <a href="/wiki/One-time_programmable" class="mw-redirect" title="One-time programmable">OTP</a>) microcontrollers are based on the Z80; they share the same basic BC,DE,HL,IX,IY register structure, and largely the same instructions, but are not binary compatible, while the previous TLCS 90 is Z80-compatible.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/NEC_78K" class="mw-redirect" title="NEC 78K">NEC 78K</a> series microcontrollers are based on the Z80; they share the same basic BC,DE,HL register structure, and has similar (but differently named) instructions; not binary compatible.</li></ul>
<dl><dt>Partly compatible</dt>
<dd></dd></dl>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Rabbit_Semiconductor" title="Rabbit Semiconductor">Rabbit Semiconductor</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Rabbit_2000" title="Rabbit 2000">Rabbit 2000/3000/4000</a> microprocessors/microcontrollers<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> are based on the <a href="/wiki/HD64180" class="mw-redirect" title="HD64180">HD64180</a>/<a href="/wiki/Z180" class="mw-redirect" title="Z180">Z180</a> architecture, although they are not fully binary compatible.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<dl><dt>No longer produced</dt>
<dd></dd></dl>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/ASCII_Corporation" title="ASCII Corporation">ASCII Corporation</a> <a href="/wiki/R800_(CPU)" title="R800 (CPU)">R800</a> was a fast 16-bit processor used in MSX <a href="/wiki/MSX#MSX_turboR" title="MSX">TurboR</a> computers; it was software, but not hardware compatible with the Z80 (signal timing, pinout &amp; function of pins differ from the Z80).</li>
<li>Zilog's NMOS <a href="/wiki/Zilog_Z800" title="Zilog Z800">Z800</a> and CMOS <a href="/wiki/Zilog_Z280" title="Zilog Z280">Z280</a> were 16-bit Z80-implementations (before the <a href="/wiki/HD64180" class="mw-redirect" title="HD64180">HD64180</a> / <a href="/wiki/Z180" class="mw-redirect" title="Z180">Z180</a>) with a 16&#160;MB paged MMU address space; they added many orthogonalizations and addressing modes to the Z80 instruction set. Minicomputer features — such as user and system modes, multiprocessor support, on chip MMU, on chip instruction and data cache and so on — were seen rather as more complexity than as functionality and support for the (usually electronics-oriented) embedded systems designer, it also made it very hard to predict instruction execution times.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li>Certain <a href="/wiki/Arcade_game" title="Arcade game">arcade games</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Pang_(video_game)" title="Pang (video game)">Pang</a>/<a href="/wiki/Buster_Bros" class="mw-redirect" title="Buster Bros">Buster Bros</a> use an encrypted "Kabuki" Z80 CPU manufactured by <a href="/wiki/VLSI_Technology" title="VLSI Technology">VLSI Technology</a>, where the decryption keys are stored in its internal <a href="/wiki/Battery-backed_memory" class="mw-redirect" title="Battery-backed memory">battery-backed memory</a>, to avoid piracy and illegal bootleg games.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_uses">Notable uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zilog_Z80&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Notable uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Desktop_computers">Desktop computers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zilog_Z80&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Desktop computers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_home_computers_by_category#Zilog_Z80_and_clones" class="mw-redirect" title="List of home computers by category">list of home computers by category §&#160;Zilog Z80 and clones</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ColecoVision-Open-FL.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/ColecoVision-Open-FL.jpg/220px-ColecoVision-Open-FL.jpg" width="220" height="111" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/ColecoVision-Open-FL.jpg/330px-ColecoVision-Open-FL.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/ColecoVision-Open-FL.jpg/440px-ColecoVision-Open-FL.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5900" data-file-height="2980" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ColecoVision-Open-FL.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Z80A was used as the CPU in a number of gaming consoles, such as this <a href="/wiki/ColecoVision" title="ColecoVision">ColecoVision</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>During the late 1970s and early 1980s, the Z80 was used in a great number of fairly anonymous business-oriented machines with the <a href="/wiki/CP/M" title="CP/M">CP/M</a> operating system, a combination that dominated the market at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Four well-known examples of Z80 business computers running CP/M are the <a href="/wiki/Heathkit_H89" class="mw-redirect" title="Heathkit H89">Heathkit H89</a>, the portable <a href="/wiki/Osborne_1" title="Osborne 1">Osborne 1</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kaypro" title="Kaypro">Kaypro</a> series, and the <a href="/wiki/Epson_QX-10" title="Epson QX-10">Epson QX-10</a>. <a href="/wiki/RM_plc" class="mw-redirect" title="RM plc">Research Machines</a> manufactured the <a href="/wiki/RM_380Z" class="mw-redirect" title="RM 380Z">380Z</a> and 480Z microcomputers which were networked with a thin Ethernet type LAN and CP/NET in 1981. Other manufacturers of such systems included <a href="/wiki/Televideo" class="mw-redirect" title="Televideo">Televideo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xerox" title="Xerox">Xerox</a> (<a href="/wiki/Xerox_820" title="Xerox 820">820 range</a>), <a href="/wiki/Sanyo" title="Sanyo">Sanyo</a> (MBC-1000/1100/1200),<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Toshiba" title="Toshiba">Toshiba</a> (T100)<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> and a number of more obscure firms such as <a href="/wiki/Altos_Computer_Systems" title="Altos Computer Systems">Altos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eagle_Computer" title="Eagle Computer">Eagle</a>. Some systems used multi-tasking operating system software (like <a href="/wiki/MP/M" title="MP/M">MP/M</a>) to share the one processor between several <a href="/wiki/Concurrent_user" title="Concurrent user">concurrent users</a>.
</p><p>In the U.S., the <a href="/wiki/Radio_Shack" class="mw-redirect" title="Radio Shack">Radio Shack</a> <a href="/wiki/TRS-80" title="TRS-80">TRS-80</a>, introduced in 1977, as well as the Models II, III, 4, and the proposed Model V, used the Z80. A number of TRS-80 clones were produced by companies like Lobo (<a href="/wiki/Max-80" title="Max-80">Max-80</a>), LNW (<a href="/wiki/LNW-80" title="LNW-80">LNW-80</a>), and Hong Kong-based EACA (<a href="/wiki/Video_Genie" title="Video Genie">Video Genie</a> and derivatives TRZ-80, PMC-80, and Dick Smith System 80). In the Netherlands a TRS-80 Model III clone was produced that had CP/M capability; this was the <a href="/wiki/Aster_CT-80" title="Aster CT-80">Aster CT-80</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Coleco_Adam" title="Coleco Adam">Coleco Adam</a> hybrid computer/game console could use <a href="/wiki/Colecovision" class="mw-redirect" title="Colecovision">Colecovision</a> games as well as CP/M. An early Z80 <a href="/wiki/Home_computer" title="Home computer">home computer</a> was the <a href="/wiki/Exidy_Sorcerer" title="Exidy Sorcerer">Exidy Sorcerer</a>.  In the United Kingdom, <a href="/wiki/Sinclair_Research" title="Sinclair Research">Sinclair Research</a> used the Z80 and Z80A in its <a href="/wiki/ZX80" title="ZX80">ZX80</a>, <a href="/wiki/ZX81" title="ZX81">ZX81</a>, and <a href="/wiki/ZX_Spectrum" title="ZX Spectrum">ZX Spectrum</a> home computers. These were marketed in the US by <a href="/wiki/Timex_Group_USA" title="Timex Group USA">Timex</a> as the Timex/Sinclair series. <a href="/wiki/Amstrad" title="Amstrad">Amstrad</a> used the Z80A in their <a href="/wiki/Amstrad_CPC" title="Amstrad CPC">Amstrad CPC</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amstrad_PCW" title="Amstrad PCW">PCW</a> ranges and an early UK computer, the <a href="/wiki/Nascom" class="mw-redirect" title="Nascom">Nascom</a> 1 and 2 also used it. The Z80 powered a great many home computers adhering to the <a href="/wiki/MSX" title="MSX">MSX</a> standard in Japan, Asia, and to a lesser extent, Europe and South America (some 5 million in Japan alone). Also in Japan <a href="/wiki/Sharp_Corporation" title="Sharp Corporation">Sharp</a> used the Z80 in its <a href="/wiki/Sharp_MZ" title="Sharp MZ">MZ</a> and <a href="/wiki/X1_(computer)" title="X1 (computer)">X1</a> series. The Hong Kong-based <a href="/wiki/VTech" title="VTech">VTech</a> made its <a href="/wiki/Laser_200" class="mw-redirect" title="Laser 200">Laser 200</a> home computer with a Z80. In Germany an Apple-CP/M hybrid called the <a href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Basis_108" class="extiw" title="de:Basis 108">Base 108</a> paired a Z80 with a 6502. Similarly the <a href="/wiki/Commodore_128" title="Commodore 128">Commodore 128</a> featured a Z80 processor alongside its <a href="/wiki/MOS_Technology_8502" title="MOS Technology 8502">MOS Technology 8502</a> processor for CP/M compatibility.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/6502_architecture_computer" class="mw-redirect" title="6502 architecture computer">6502 architecture computers</a> on the market at the time, such as the <a href="/wiki/BBC_Micro" title="BBC Micro">BBC Micro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apple_II" title="Apple II">Apple II</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/MOS_Technology_6510" title="MOS Technology 6510">6510</a> based <a href="/wiki/Commodore_64" title="Commodore 64">Commodore 64</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> could make use of the Z80 with an external unit, a plug-in card, or an expansion <a href="/wiki/ROM_cartridge" title="ROM cartridge">ROM cartridge</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> <a href="/wiki/Z-80_SoftCard" title="Z-80 SoftCard">Z-80 SoftCard</a> for the Apple II was a particularly successful add-on card and one of Microsoft's few hardware products of the era.
</p><p>In 1981, Multitech (later to become <a href="/wiki/Acer_Inc." title="Acer Inc.">Acer</a>) introduced the <a href="/wiki/Microprofessor_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Microprofessor I">Microprofessor I</a>, a simple and inexpensive training system for the Z80 microprocessor. Currently, it is still manufactured and sold by Flite Electronics International Limited in <a href="/wiki/Southampton,_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Southampton, England">Southampton, England</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ZX_Spectrum.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/ZX_Spectrum.jpg/220px-ZX_Spectrum.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/ZX_Spectrum.jpg/330px-ZX_Spectrum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/ZX_Spectrum.jpg/440px-ZX_Spectrum.jpg 2x" data-file-width="773" data-file-height="519" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ZX_Spectrum.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Sinclair_Research" title="Sinclair Research">Sinclair</a> <a href="/wiki/ZX_Spectrum" title="ZX Spectrum">ZX Spectrum</a> which used a Z80 Clocked at 3.5 MHz</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Embedded_systems_and_consumer_electronics">Embedded systems and consumer electronics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zilog_Z80&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Embedded systems and consumer electronics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Zilog_Z80&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(March 2018)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PABX.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/PABX.jpg/220px-PABX.jpg" width="220" height="309" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/PABX.jpg/330px-PABX.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/PABX.jpg/440px-PABX.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1960" data-file-height="2752" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PABX.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Z80-based <a href="/wiki/PABX" class="mw-redirect" title="PABX">PABX</a>. The Z80 is third chip in from the left, to the right of the chip with the hand-written white label on it.</div></div></div>
<p>The Zilog Z80 has long been a popular microprocessor in <a href="/wiki/Embedded_system" title="Embedded system">embedded systems</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microcontroller" title="Microcontroller">microcontroller</a> cores,<sup id="cite_ref-esd_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-esd-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> where it remains in widespread use today.<sup id="cite_ref-popularity_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popularity-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-robertson_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-robertson-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The following list provides examples of such applications of the Z80, including uses in <a href="/wiki/Consumer_electronic" class="mw-redirect" title="Consumer electronic">consumer electronics</a> products.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry">Industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zilog_Z80&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> <a href="/wiki/PC-XT" class="mw-redirect" title="PC-XT">PC-XT</a>'s hard drive controller, which was actually an IBM adaptation of the Xebec 1410 <a href="/wiki/Shugart_Associates_System_Interface#History" class="mw-redirect" title="Shugart Associates System Interface">SASI</a> controller, but on a PC-XT bus, not a SASI bus.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Office_equipment" class="mw-redirect" title="Office equipment">Office equipment</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Matrix_printer" class="mw-redirect" title="Matrix printer">matrix printers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fax_machine" class="mw-redirect" title="Fax machine">fax machines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Answering_machine" title="Answering machine">answering machines</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Photocopying" class="mw-redirect" title="Photocopying">photocopiers</a> are known examples. The original Hewlett-Packard <a href="/wiki/Deskjet" class="mw-redirect" title="Deskjet">Deskjet</a> printer used a Z180.</li>
<li>Industrial <a href="/wiki/Programmable_logic_controller" title="Programmable logic controller">programmable logic controllers</a> (PLCs) use the Z80 in CPU modules, for auxiliary functions such as analog I/O, or in communication modules.</li>
<li>It has also been employed in robots, for example for speech recognition<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> and low level tasks such as <a href="/wiki/Servo_drive" title="Servo drive">servo processors</a> in pick and place machines.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/RS-232" title="RS-232">RS-232</a> multiplexers connecting large numbers of old style "<a href="/wiki/Computer_terminal" title="Computer terminal">terminals</a>" to minicomputers or mainframes used arrays of Z80 CPU/SIO boards.</li>
<li>Applications such as TV <a href="/wiki/Broadcasting" title="Broadcasting">broadcast</a> <a href="/wiki/Vision_mixer" title="Vision mixer">vision mixers</a> have used the Z80 for embedded <a href="/wiki/Real-time_computing" title="Real-time computing">real time</a> subtasks.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>It has also been used in <a href="/wiki/Seagate_Technology" title="Seagate Technology">Seagate Technology</a>'s and other manufacturers' <a href="/wiki/Hard_disk" class="mw-redirect" title="Hard disk">hard disks</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Credit_card" title="Credit card">Credit card</a> consoles controlling fuel pumps used Z80 CPU and PIOs (US patents 4930665,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> 4962462,<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> and 5602745<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>).</li>
<li>Retail industry point-of-sale <a href="/wiki/Credit_card_terminal" class="mw-redirect" title="Credit card terminal">credit card terminals</a> manufactured by <a href="/wiki/VeriFone" class="mw-redirect" title="VeriFone">VeriFone</a> used the Z80 processor.</li>
<li>Several PC <a href="/wiki/Expansion_card" title="Expansion card">expansion cards</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Adaptec" title="Adaptec">Adaptecs</a> <a href="/wiki/SCSI" title="SCSI">SCSI</a> boards, have been using the Z80/Z180 and peripheral chips.</li>
<li>Z80/Z180/Z380 have been used in <a href="/wiki/Telecommunication" title="Telecommunication">telecommunication</a> equipment such as <a href="/wiki/Telephone_switch" class="mw-redirect" title="Telephone switch">telephone switches</a> and various kinds of <a href="/wiki/Modem" title="Modem">modems</a>.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Stofor" title="Stofor">Stofor</a> message switch, used extensively by banks and brokers in the UK was Z80 based.</li>
<li>Cash registers and store management systems</li>
<li>Home automation, wireless sprinkler control and wireless mesh using the N8VEM open source homebrew system.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Breathalyzer" title="Breathalyzer">Breathalyzer</a> equipment used by law enforcement agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumer_electronics">Consumer electronics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zilog_Z80&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Consumer electronics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Amstrad_NC100" title="Amstrad NC100">Amstrad NC100/NC200</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Z88" title="Cambridge Z88">Cambridge Z88</a> notebook computers made in the United Kingdom used the Z80.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Amstrad" title="Amstrad">Amstrad</a> in the UK produced an early <a href="/wiki/Personal_digital_assistant" title="Personal digital assistant">PDA</a> called the <a href="/wiki/PenPad" title="PenPad">PenPad</a>, or PDA600, which was built around a 14&#160;MHz Z180.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Sinclair_Research" title="Sinclair Research">Sinclair</a> <a href="/wiki/ZX80" title="ZX80">ZX80</a>, <a href="/wiki/ZX81" title="ZX81">ZX81</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/ZX_Spectrum" title="ZX Spectrum">ZX Spectrum</a> had a Z80 Processor, <a href="/wiki/ZX80" title="ZX80">ZX80</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/ZX81" title="ZX81">ZX81</a> had the Processor Clocked at 3.25&#160;MHz while the <a href="/wiki/ZX_Spectrum" title="ZX Spectrum">ZX Spectrum</a> was Clocked at 3.5&#160;MHz.</li>
<li>Hong Kong-based <a href="/wiki/VTech" title="VTech">VTech</a> used Z80s in a line of sub-notebook and educational computers. The last and most advanced of which were the Laser PC4,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> PC5,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> and PC6.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sharp_Corporation" title="Sharp Corporation">Sharp</a> produced a series of pocket computers based on custom processors that were Z80 compatible. Examples include <a href="/wiki/PC-1500" class="mw-redirect" title="PC-1500">PC-1500</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sharp_PC-1600" title="Sharp PC-1600">PC-1600</a> and <a href="/wiki/PC-E220" class="mw-redirect" title="PC-E220">PC-E220</a>.</li>
<li>Various <a href="/wiki/Scientific_calculator" title="Scientific calculator">scientific</a> and <a href="/wiki/Graphing_calculator" title="Graphing calculator">graphing calculators</a> use the Z80, including the <a href="/wiki/Texas_Instruments" title="Texas Instruments">Texas Instruments</a> <a href="/wiki/TI-73" class="mw-redirect" title="TI-73">TI-73</a>, <a href="/wiki/TI-81" title="TI-81">TI-81</a>, <a href="/wiki/TI-82" title="TI-82">TI-82</a>, <a href="/wiki/TI-83_series" title="TI-83 series">TI-83</a>, <a href="/wiki/TI-83_series#TI-83_Plus" title="TI-83 series">TI-83+</a>, <a href="/wiki/TI-84_Plus_series" title="TI-84 Plus series">TI-84+</a>, <a href="/wiki/TI-85" title="TI-85">TI-85</a> and <a href="/wiki/TI-86" title="TI-86">TI-86</a> series.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Z80 was often used in <a href="/wiki/Coin-operated" class="mw-redirect" title="Coin-operated">coin-operated</a> <a href="/wiki/Arcade_game" title="Arcade game">arcade games</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-popularity_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-popularity-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and was commonly used as the main CPU, sound or <a href="/wiki/Video_coprocessor" class="mw-redirect" title="Video coprocessor">video coprocessors</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pac-Man" title="Pac-Man">Pac-Man</a> arcade games feature a single Z80 as the main CPU,<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Frogger" title="Frogger">Frogger</a> used two Z80 CPUs. <a href="/wiki/Galaxian" title="Galaxian">Galaxian</a> and arcade games such as <a href="/wiki/King_%26_Balloon" title="King &amp; Balloon">King &amp; Balloon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Check_Man" title="Check Man">Check Man</a> that use the <a href="/wiki/Namco_Galaxian" title="Namco Galaxian">Namco Galaxian</a> boardset also use a Z80 as the main CPU.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Other <a href="/wiki/Namco" title="Namco">Namco</a> licensed arcade games such as <a href="/wiki/Galaga" title="Galaga">Galaga</a> and other games that use the <a href="/wiki/Namco_Galaga" title="Namco Galaga">Namco Galaga</a> boardset such as <a href="/wiki/Bosconian" title="Bosconian">Bosconian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dig_Dug" title="Dig Dug">Dig Dug</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xevious" title="Xevious">Xevious</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Super_Xevious" title="Super Xevious">Super Xevious</a> use three Z80 microprocessors running in parallel for the main CPU, graphics, and sound.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> Later <a href="/wiki/Sega" title="Sega">Sega</a> System 16, 18, X, and Y; <a href="/wiki/Capcom" title="Capcom">Capcom</a> CPS1 and CPS2; <a href="/wiki/SNK_Playmore" class="mw-redirect" title="SNK Playmore">SNK</a> Neo Geo MVS use 68000 and Z80 combination as co processors or sound CPUs.</li>
<li>It was also found in home video game consoles such as the <a href="/wiki/ColecoVision" title="ColecoVision">ColecoVision</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sega_Master_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Sega Master System">Sega Master System</a><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sega_Game_Gear" class="mw-redirect" title="Sega Game Gear">Sega Game Gear</a> <a href="/wiki/Video_game_console" title="Video game console">video game consoles</a>, as an audio and general-purpose co-processor in the <a href="/wiki/Sega_Genesis" title="Sega Genesis">Sega Genesis</a> and as an audio controller and co-processor to the Motorola 68000 in the <a href="/wiki/SNK_Playmore" class="mw-redirect" title="SNK Playmore">SNK</a> <a href="/wiki/Neo_Geo_(console)" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo Geo (console)">Neo-Geo</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nintendo" title="Nintendo">Nintendo</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Game_Boy" title="Game Boy">Game Boy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Game_Boy_Color" title="Game Boy Color">Game Boy Color</a> handheld game systems used an 8080-derived processor with some Z80 instructions added (CB prefix) as well as unique auto-increment/decrement addressing modes. The CPU was a <a href="/wiki/Sharp_Corporation" title="Sharp Corporation">Sharp Corporation</a> LR35902<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> running at 4.19&#160;MHz in the original and Pocket models, and 8.39&#160;MHz in the Color model. This processor was later included in the <a href="/wiki/Game_Boy_Advance" title="Game Boy Advance">Game Boy Advance</a> / SP / Micro taking up a new role as a co-processor for backwards compatibility with Game Boy / Color games (except Micro) and to add legacy 8-bit sounds to supplement the digital samples in Game Boy Advance games.</li>
<li>The <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ericsson_GA628&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ericsson GA628 (page does not exist)">Ericsson GA628</a> mobile phone uses the Z80 CPU.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In Russia, Z80 and its clones were widely used in multi-functional land line phones with Caller ID.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li>All the <a href="/wiki/S1_MP3_player" title="S1 MP3 player">S1 MP3 player</a> type digital audio players use the Z80 instruction set.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_instruments">Musical instruments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Zilog_Z80&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Musical instruments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/MIDI" title="MIDI">MIDI</a> sequencers such as <a href="/wiki/E-mu" class="mw-redirect" title="E-mu">E-mu</a> <i>4060 Polyphonic Keyboard and Sequencer</i>, <i>Zyklus MPS</i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Roland_Corporation" title="Roland Corporation">Roland</a> MSQ700</i> were built around the Z80.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/MIDI" title="MIDI">MIDI</a> controllers and switches such as <i>Waldorf Midi-Bay MB-15</i> and others.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup></li>
<li>Several <a href="/wiki/Polyphonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyphonic">polyphonic</a> <a href="/wiki/Analog_synthesizer" title="Analog synthesizer">analog synthesizers</a> used it for keyboard-scanning (also wheels, knobs, displays...) and <a href="/wiki/D/A" class="mw-redirect" title="D/A">D/A</a> or <a href="/wiki/Pulse-width_modulation" title="Pulse-width modulation">PWM</a> control of analog levels; in newer designs, sometimes sequencing or <a href="/wiki/MIDI" title="MIDI">MIDI</a>-communication. The Z80 was also often involved in the sound generation itself, implementing <a href="/wiki/Low-frequency_oscillator" class="mw-redirect" title="Low-frequency oscillator">LFOs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Envelope_generator" class="mw-redirect" title="Envelope generator">envelope generators</a> and so on. Known examples include:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Sequential_Circuits" class="mw-redirect" title="Sequential Circuits">Sequential Circuits</a> <a href="/wiki/Prophet_5" class="mw-redirect" title="Prophet 5">Prophet 5</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Prophet_10&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Prophet 10 (page does not exist)">Prophet 10</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Prophet_T8&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Prophet T8 (page does not exist)">Prophet T8</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Prophet_600&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Prophet 600 (page does not exist)">Prophet 600</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Six-Trak&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Six-Trak (page does not exist)">Six-Trak</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Multitrak&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Multitrak (page does not exist)">Multitrak</a>, MAX, and <a href="/wiki/Split-8" title="Split-8">Split-8</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Memorymoog" title="Memorymoog">MemoryMoog</a> six-voice synthesizer<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Oberheim_OB-8" title="Oberheim OB-8">Oberheim OB-8</a> eight-voice synthesizer with <a href="/wiki/MIDI" title="MIDI">MIDI</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Roland_Jupiter-8" title="Roland Jupiter-8">Roland Jupiter-8</a> eight-voice synthesizer</li>
<li>VEM Tiracon 6V six-voice analog synthesizer (1987) with U880 CPU from East Germany (Deutsche Demokratische Republik)</li></ul></li>
<li>Digital <a href="/wiki/Sampling_(signal_processing)" title="Sampling (signal processing)">sampling</a> <a href="/wiki/Synthesizer" title="Synthesizer">synthesizers</a> such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Emulator_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Emulator I">Emulator I</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Emulator_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Emulator II">Emulator II</a></i>, and <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Akai_S700&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Akai S700 (page does not exist)">Akai S700</a> 12-bit Sampler</i>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Drum_machine" title="Drum machine">drum machines</a> like the <i>E-mu SP-12</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/E-mu_SP-1200" title="E-mu SP-1200">E-mu SP-1200</a></i>, <i>E-mu Drumulator</i>, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Sequential_Circuits" class="mw-redirect" title="Sequential Circuits">Sequential Circuits</a> Drumtraks</i>, used Z80 processors.</li>
<li>Many <a href="/wiki/Lexicon_(company)" title="Lexicon (company)">Lexicon</a> reverberators (<a href="/wiki/PCM70" class="mw-redirect" title="PCM70">PCM70</a>, LXP15, LXP1, MPX100) used one or more Z80s for user interface and <a href="/wiki/Low-frequency_oscillator" class="mw-redirect" title="Low-frequency oscillator">LFO</a> generation where dedicated hardware provided <a href="/wiki/Digital_signal_processing" title="Digital signal processing">DSP</a> functions.</li>
<li>The ADA MP-1 and Digitech GSP 2101/2112/2120, MIDI controlled, <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">vacuum tube</a>, guitar pre-amplifiers.</li></ul>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-Electronics_Aug_1976-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Electronics_Aug_1976_12-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal"><a href="/wiki/Masatoshi_Shima" title="Masatoshi Shima">Shima, Masatoshi</a>; Federico Faggin; Ralph Ungermann (August 19, 1976). <li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See Federico Faggin, oral history.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Mathur. <i>Introduction to Microprocessors</i>. p.&#160;111. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-07-460222-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-07-460222-5">978-0-07-460222-5</a>. <q>The register architecture of the Z80 is more innovative than that of the 8085</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Introduction+to+Microprocessors&amp;rft.pages=111&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-07-460222-5&amp;rft.au=Mathur&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AZilog+Z80" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFCiarcia1981">Ciarcia 1981</a>, pp.&#160;31,32</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Although the 8080 had 16-bit addition and 16-bit <a href="/w/index.php?title=Increment_and_decrement_instructions&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Increment and decrement instructions (page does not exist)">increment and decrement instructions</a>, it had no explicit 16-bit subtraction, and no overflow flag. The Z80 complemented this with the ADC HL,rr and SBC HL,rr instructions which sets the new overflow flag accordingly. (The 8080 compatible ADD HL,rr does not.)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-interruptmodes-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-interruptmodes_17-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-interruptmodes_17-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Wai-Kai Chen (2002). <i>The circuits and filters handbook</i>. <a href="/wiki/CRC_Press" title="CRC Press">CRC Press</a>. p.&#160;1943. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-8493-0912-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-8493-0912-0">978-0-8493-0912-0</a>. <q>interrupt processing commences according to the interrupt method stipulated by the IM <i>i</i>, <i>i</i>=0, 1, or 2, instruction. If <i>i</i>=1, for direct method, the PC is loaded with 0038H. If <i>i</i>=0, for vectored method, the interrupting device has the opportunity to place the op-code for one byte . If <i>i</i>=2, for indirect vector method, the interrupting device must then place a byte . The Z80 then uses this byte where one of 128 interrupt vectors can be selected by the byte .</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+circuits+and+filters+handbook&amp;rft.pages=1943&amp;rft.pub=CRC+Press&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-8493-0912-0&amp;rft.au=Wai-Kai+Chen&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AZilog+Z80" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Notably to simultaneously handle the 32-bit <a href="/wiki/Significand" title="Significand">mantissas</a> of two <a href="/wiki/Operand" title="Operand">operands</a> in the 40-bit <a href="/wiki/Floating_point" class="mw-redirect" title="Floating point">floating point</a> format used in the <a href="/wiki/Sinclair_ZX81" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinclair ZX81">Sinclair</a> home computers. They were also used in a similar fashion in some earlier but lesser known Z80 based computers, such as the Swedish <a href="/wiki/ABC_80" title="ABC 80">ABC 80</a> and <a href="/wiki/ABC_800" title="ABC 800">ABC 800</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As this refresh does not need to transfer any data, just output sequential row-addresses, it occupies less than 1.5 T-states. The dedicated M1-signal (<i>machine cycle one</i>) in the Z80 can be used to allow memory chips the same amount of access time for instruction fetches as for data access, i.e almost two full T-states out of the 4T <i>fetch</i> cycle (as well as out of the 3T data <i>read</i> cycle). The Z80 could use memory with the same range of access times as the 8080 (or the 8086) at the same clock frequency. This long M1-signal (relative to the clock) also meant that the Z80 could employ about 4-5 times the internal frequency of a 6800, 6502 or similar using the same type of memory.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.primrosebank.net/computers/z80/z80_special_reset.htm">"Z80 Special Reset"</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Z80+Special+Reset&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.primrosebank.net%2Fcomputers%2Fz80%2Fz80_special_reset.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AZilog+Z80" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Jump (<code>JP</code>) instructions, which load the program counter with a new instruction address, do not themselves access memory. Absolute and relative forms of the jump reflect this by omitting the round brackets from their operands. Register based jump instructions such as "<code>JP (HL)</code>" include round brackets in an apparent deviation from this convention.<cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://z80cpu.eu/mirrors/oldcomputers.dyndns.org/manuals/z80asm.pdf">"Z80 Relocating Macro Assembler User's Guide"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. p.&#160;B–2.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Z80+Relocating+Macro+Assembler+User%27s+Guide&amp;rft.pages=B-2&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fz80cpu.eu%2Fmirrors%2Foldcomputers.dyndns.org%2Fmanuals%2Fz80asm.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AZilog+Z80" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-undocz80-44"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-undocz80_44-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-undocz80_44-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Bot, Jacco J. T. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.z80.info/z80undoc.htm">"Z80 Undocumented Instructions"</a>. <i>Home of the Z80 CPU</i>. <q>If an opcode works with the registers HL, H or L then if that opcode is preceded by #DD (or #FD) it works on IX, IXH or IXL (or IY, IYH, IYL), with some exceptions. The exceptions are instructions like LD H,IXH and LD L,IYH</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Home+of+the+Z80+CPU&amp;rft.atitle=Z80+Undocumented+Instructions&amp;rft.aulast=Bot&amp;rft.aufirst=Jacco+J.+T.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.z80.info%2Fz80undoc.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AZilog+Z80" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-55"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-55">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Young, Sean (1998). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://z80.info/z80undoc3.txt">"Z80 Undocumented Features (in software behaviour)"</a>. <q>The I/O instructions use the whole of the address bus, not just the lower 8 bits. So in fact, you can have 65536 I/O ports in a Z80 system (the Spectrum uses this). IN r,(C), OUT (C),r and all the I/O block instructions put the whole of BC on the address bus. IN A,(n) and OUT (n),A put A*256+n on the address bus.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Z80+Undocumented+Features+%28in+software+behaviour%29&amp;rft.date=1998&amp;rft.aulast=Young&amp;rft.aufirst=Sean&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fz80.info%2Fz80undoc3.txt&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AZilog+Z80" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/></span>
